<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_neptunium" title="Template:Infobox neptunium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_neptunium" title="Template talk:Infobox neptunium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_neptunium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Neptunium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Np</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 93. A <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactivity">radioactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> metal, neptunium is the first <a href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Transuranic element">transuranic element</a>. Its position in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> just after <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, named after the planet <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, led to it being named after <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, the next planet beyond Uranus. A neptunium atom has 93 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and 93 electrons, of which seven are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. Neptunium metal is silvery and <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> when exposed to air. The element occurs in three <a href="/wiki/Allotrope" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotrope">allotropic</a> forms and it normally exhibits five <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a>, ranging from +3 to +7. It is <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiation poisoning">poisonous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a>, and can accumulate in <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, which makes the handling of neptunium dangerous.
</p><p>Although many false claims of its discovery were made over the years, the element was first synthesized by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_McMillan" title="Edwin McMillan">Edwin McMillan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_H._Abelson" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip H. Abelson">Philip H. Abelson</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Radiation_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Berkeley Radiation Laboratory">Berkeley Radiation Laboratory</a> in 1940. Since then, most neptunium has been and still is produced by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_irradiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron irradiation">neutron irradiation</a> of uranium in nuclear reactors. The vast majority is generated as a by-product in conventional <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> reactors. While neptunium itself has no commercial uses at present, it is used as a precursor for the formation of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>, used in <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermal_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope thermal generator">radioisotope thermal generators</a> to provide electricity for <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>. Neptunium has also been used in <a href="/wiki/Neutron_detector" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron detector">detectors</a> of high-energy <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>.
</p><p>The most stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of neptunium, neptunium-237, is a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> production. It, and the isotope neptunium-239, are also found in trace amounts in <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> ores due to <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture reactions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neptunium is a <a href="/wiki/Hardness_(materials_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardness (materials science)">hard</a>, silvery, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactivity">radioactive</a> actinide <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, it is located to the right of the actinide <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, to the left of the actinide <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> and below the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida718_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida718-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Neptunium is a hard metal, having a bulk modulus of 118&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a>, comparable to that of <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Neptunium metal is similar to uranium in terms of physical workability. When exposed to air at normal temperatures, it forms a thin oxide layer. This reaction proceeds more rapidly as the temperature increases.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida718_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida718-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Neptunium has been determined to melt at 639±3&#160;°C: this low melting point, a property the metal shares with the neighboring element plutonium (which has melting point 639.4&#160;°C), is due to the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital hybridization">hybridization</a> of the 5f and 6d orbitals and the formation of directional bonds in the metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The boiling point of neptunium is not empirically known and the usually given value of 4174&#160;°C is extrapolated from the <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> of the element. If accurate, this would give neptunium the largest liquid range of any element (3535&#160;K passes between its melting and boiling points).<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida718_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida718-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gray_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gray-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neptunium is found in at least three <a href="/wiki/Allotrope" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotrope">allotropes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Some claims of a fourth allotrope have been made, but they are so far not proven.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida718_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida718-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This multiplicity of allotropes is common among the actinides. The <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structures</a> of neptunium, <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a>, uranium, and plutonium do not have clear analogs among the lanthanides and are more similar to those of the 3d&#160;<a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td><i>a</i> = 351.8
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_(1975).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_%281975%29.png/240px-Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_%281975%29.png" width="240" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_%281975%29.png/360px-Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_%281975%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_%281975%29.png/480px-Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_%281975%29.png 2x" data-file-width="823" data-file-height="850" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_neptunium_(1975).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phase diagram of neptunium</div></div></div>
<p>α-neptunium takes on an <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthorhombic">orthorhombic</a> structure, resembling a highly distorted body-centered cubic structure.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Each neptunium atom is coordinated to four others and the Np–Np bond lengths are 260&#160;pm.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida719_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida719-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is the densest of all the actinides and the fifth-densest of all naturally occurring elements, behind only <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gray_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gray-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> α-neptunium has <a href="/wiki/Semimetal" title="Semimetal">semimetallic</a> properties, such as strong <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonding</a> and a high <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">electrical resistivity</a>, and its metallic physical properties are closer to those of the <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a> than the true metals. Some allotropes of the other actinides also exhibit similar behaviour, though to a lesser degree.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The densities of different isotopes of neptunium in the alpha phase are expected to be observably different: α-<sup>235</sup>Np should have density 20.303&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>; α-<sup>236</sup>Np, density 20.389&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>; α-<sup>237</sup>Np, density 20.476&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-critical_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critical-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>β-neptunium takes on a distorted tetragonal close-packed structure. Four atoms of neptunium make up a unit cell, and the Np–Np bond lengths are 276&#160;pm.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida719_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida719-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> γ-neptunium has a <a href="/wiki/Body-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Body-centered cubic">body-centered cubic</a> structure and has Np–Np bond length of 297&#160;pm. The γ form becomes less stable with increased pressure, though the melting point of neptunium also increases with pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida719_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida719-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The β-Np/γ-Np/liquid <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> occurs at 725&#160;°C and 3200&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida719_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida719-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to the presence of valence 5f electrons, neptunium and its alloys exhibit very interesting magnetic behavior, like many other actinides. These can range from the itinerant band-like character characteristic of the <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a> to the local moment behavior typical of <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>. This stems from 5f-orbital hybridization with the orbitals of the metal <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>, and the fact that the 5f orbital is <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="Relativistic effects">relativistically</a> destabilized and extends outwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida720_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida720-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> For example, pure neptunium is <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a>, Np<a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Al</a><sub>3</sub> is <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a>, Np<a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">Ge</a><sub>3</sub> has no magnetic ordering, and Np<a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">Sn</a><sub>3</sub> behaves <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermionically</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida720_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida720-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Investigations are underway regarding alloys of neptunium with uranium, <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, so as to recycle long-lived waste isotopes such as neptunium-237 into shorter-lived isotopes more useful as nuclear fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida720_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida720-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One neptunium-based <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductor</a> alloy has been discovered with formula Np<a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">Pd</a><sub>5</sub>Al<sub>2</sub>. This occurrence in neptunium compounds is somewhat surprising because they often exhibit strong magnetism, which usually destroys superconductivity. The alloy has a tetragonal structure with a superconductivity transition temperature of −268.3&#160;°C (4.9&#160;K).<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neptunium has five ionic <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> ranging from +3 to +7 when forming chemical compounds, which can be simultaneously observed in solutions. It is the heaviest actinide that can lose all its valence electrons in a stable compound. The most stable state in solution is +5, but the valence +4 is preferred in solid neptunium compounds. Neptunium metal is very reactive. Ions of neptunium are prone to hydrolysis and formation of <a href="/wiki/Coordination_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Coordination compound">coordination compounds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Himiya_neptuniya_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Himiya_neptuniya-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic">Atomic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Atomic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A neptunium atom has 93 electrons, arranged in the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">configuration</a> [<a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">Rn</a>]5f<sup>4</sup>6d<sup>1</sup>7s<sup>2</sup>. This differs from the configuration expected by the <a href="/wiki/Aufbau_principle" title="Aufbau principle">Aufbau principle</a> in that one electron is in the 6d subshell instead of being as expected in the 5f subshell. This is because of the similarity of the electron energies of the 5f, 6d, and 7s subshells. In forming compounds and ions, all the valence electrons may be lost, leaving behind an inert core of inner electrons with the electron configuration of the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> radon;<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> more commonly, only some of the valence electrons will be lost. The electron configuration for the tripositive ion Np<sup>3+</sup> is [Rn]&#160;5f<sup>4</sup>, with the outermost 7s and 6d electrons lost first: this is exactly analogous to neptunium's lanthanide homolog promethium, and conforms to the trend set by the other actinides with their [Rn]&#160;5f<sup><i>n</i></sup> electron configurations in the tripositive state. The first <a href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionization potential">ionization potential</a> of neptunium was measured to be at most <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000619000000000000♠</span>6.19<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.12&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a></span> in 1974, based on the assumption that the 7s electrons would ionize before 5f and 6d;<sup id="cite_ref-NIST_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIST-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> more recent measurements have refined this to 6.2657&#160;eV.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_neptunium" title="Isotopes of neptunium">Isotopes of neptunium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Decay_Chain(4n%2B1,_Neptunium_Series).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Decay_Chain%284n%2B1%2C_Neptunium_Series%29.svg/310px-Decay_Chain%284n%2B1%2C_Neptunium_Series%29.svg.png" width="310" height="620" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Decay_Chain%284n%2B1%2C_Neptunium_Series%29.svg/465px-Decay_Chain%284n%2B1%2C_Neptunium_Series%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Decay_Chain%284n%2B1%2C_Neptunium_Series%29.svg/620px-Decay_Chain%284n%2B1%2C_Neptunium_Series%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1310" data-file-height="2620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Decay_Chain(4n%2B1,_Neptunium_Series).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 4<i>n</i> + 1 <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of neptunium-237, commonly called the "neptunium series"</div></div></div>
<p>20 neptunium <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized with the most stable being <sup>237</sup>Np with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 2.14 million years, <sup>236</sup>Np with a half-life of 154,000 years, and <sup>235</sup>Np with a half-life of 396.1 days.  All of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are less than 4.5 days, and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than 50 minutes. This element also has at least four <a href="/wiki/Meta_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Meta state">meta states</a>, with the most stable being <sup>236m</sup>Np with a half-life of 22.5 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-unc_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unc-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The isotopes of neptunium range in <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> from 225.0339 <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic mass unit">u</a> (<sup>225</sup>Np) to 244.068 u (<sup>244</sup>Np).<sup id="cite_ref-unc_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unc-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Most of the isotopes that are lighter than the most stable one, <sup>237</sup>Np, <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">decay</a> primarily by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> although a sizable number, most notably <sup>229</sup>Np and <sup>230</sup>Np, also exhibit various levels of decay via <a href="/wiki/Alpha_emission" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha emission">alpha emission</a> to become <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a>. <sup>237</sup>Np itself, being the <a href="/wiki/Beta-decay_stable_isobars" title="Beta-decay stable isobars">beta-stable isobar</a> of mass number 237, decays almost exclusively by alpha emission into <sup>233</sup><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_protactinium" title="Isotopes of protactinium">Pa</a>, with very rare (occurring only about once in trillions of decays) <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cluster_decay" title="Cluster decay">cluster decay</a> (emission of <sup>30</sup>Mg to form <sup>207</sup>Tl). All of the known isotopes except one that are heavier than this decay exclusively via <a href="/wiki/Beta_emission" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta emission">beta emission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-unc_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unc-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The lone exception, <sup>240m</sup>Np, exhibits a rare (&gt;0.12%) decay by <a href="/wiki/Isomeric_transition" class="mw-redirect" title="Isomeric transition">isomeric transition</a> in addition to the beta emission.<sup id="cite_ref-unc_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unc-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <sup>237</sup>Np eventually decays to form <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>-209 and <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>-205, unlike most other common heavy nuclei which decay into <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lead" title="Isotopes of lead">isotopes of lead</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> is known as the <a href="/wiki/Neptunium_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Neptunium series">neptunium series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> This decay chain had long been extinct on Earth due to the short half-lives of all of its isotopes above bismuth-209, but is now being resurrected thanks to artificial production of neptunium on the tonne scale.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The isotopes neptunium-235, -236, and -237 are predicted to be <a href="/wiki/Fissile" class="mw-redirect" title="Fissile">fissile</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-critical_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critical-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> only neptunium-237's fissionability has been experimentally shown, with the <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a> being about 60&#160;kg, only about 10&#160;kg more than that of the commonly used <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Weiss_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Calculated values of the critical masses of neptunium-235, -236, and -237 respectively are 66.2&#160;kg, 6.79&#160;kg, and 63.6&#160;kg: the neptunium-236 value is even lower than that of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>. In particular <sup>236</sup>Np also has a low neutron <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-critical_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critical-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, a neptunium <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a> has never been built:<sup id="cite_ref-Weiss_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> uranium and plutonium have lower critical masses than <sup>235</sup>Np and <sup>237</sup>Np, and <sup>236</sup>Np is difficult to purify as it is not found in quantity in <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a><sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and is nearly impossible to separate in any significant quantities from its parent <sup>237</sup>Np.<sup id="cite_ref-Jukka_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jukka-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since all isotopes of neptunium have half-lives that are many times shorter than the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a>, any primordial neptunium should have decayed by now. After only about 80 million years, the concentration of even the longest lived isotope, <sup>237</sup>Np, would have been reduced to less than one-trillionth (10<sup>−12</sup>) of its original amount;<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida704_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida704-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and even if the whole Earth had initially been made of pure <sup>237</sup>Np (and ignoring that this would be well over its <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a> of 60&#160;kg), 2100 half-lives would have passed since the <a href="/wiki/Formation_of_the_Solar_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Formation of the Solar System">formation of the Solar System</a>, and thus all of it would have decayed. Thus neptunium is present in nature only in negligible amounts produced as intermediate decay products of other isotopes.<sup id="cite_ref-Himiya_neptuniya_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Himiya_neptuniya-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">Trace</a> amounts of the neptunium isotopes neptunium-237 and -239 are found naturally as <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a> from  <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> reactions in <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-emsley345347_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley345347-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In particular, <sup>239</sup>Np and <sup>237</sup>Np are the most common of these isotopes; they are directly formed from <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> by uranium-238 atoms. These neutrons come from the <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> of uranium-238, naturally neutron-induced fission of uranium-235, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a> of nuclei, and light elements absorbing <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> and emitting a neutron.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida704_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida704-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The half-life of <sup>239</sup>Np is very short, although the detection of its much longer-lived <a href="/wiki/Daughter_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Daughter product">daughter</a> <sup>239</sup>Pu in nature in 1951 definitively established its natural occurrence.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida704_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida704-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In 1952, <sup>237</sup>Np was identified and isolated from concentrates of uranium ore from the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a>: in these minerals, the ratio of neptunium-237 to uranium is less than or equal to about 10<sup>−12</sup> to 1.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida704_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida704-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thompson1to4_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thompson1to4-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most neptunium (and plutonium) now encountered in the environment is due to atmospheric nuclear explosions that took place between the detonation of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity test">first atomic bomb</a> in 1945 and the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a> in 1963. The total amount of neptunium released by these explosions and the few atmospheric tests that have been carried out since 1963 is estimated to be around 2500&#160;kg. The overwhelming majority of this is composed of the long-lived isotopes <sup>236</sup>Np and <sup>237</sup>Np since even the moderately long-lived <sup>235</sup>Np (half-life 396&#160;days) would have decayed to less than one-billionth (10<sup>−9</sup>) its original concentration over the intervening decades. An additional very small amount of neptunium, created by neutron irradiation of natural uranium in nuclear reactor cooling water, is released when the water is discharged into rivers or lakes.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida704_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida704-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thompson1to4_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thompson1to4-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The concentration of <sup>237</sup>Np in seawater is approximately 6.5&#160;×&#160;10<sup>−5</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Becquerel_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Becquerel (unit)">millibecquerels</a> per <a href="/wiki/Liter" class="mw-redirect" title="Liter">liter</a>: this concentration is between 0.1% and 1% that of plutonium.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida704_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida704-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Once in the environment, neptunium generally <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidizes</a> fairly quickly, usually to the +4 or +5 state. Regardless of its oxidation state, the element exhibits a much greater mobility than the other actinides, largely due to its ability to readily form aqueous solutions with various other elements. In one study comparing the diffusion rates of neptunium(V), plutonium(IV), and americium(III) in sandstone and limestone, neptunium penetrated more than ten times as well as the other elements. Np(V) will also react efficiently in pH levels greater than 5.5 if there are no <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> present and in these conditions it has also been observed to readily bond with <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>. It has also been observed to bond well with <a href="/wiki/Goethite" title="Goethite">goethite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferric_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferric oxide">ferric oxide</a> colloids, and several clays including <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smectite" class="mw-redirect" title="Smectite">smectite</a>. Np(V) does not bond as readily to soil particles in mildly acidic conditions as its fellow actinides americium and curium by nearly an order of magnitude. This behavior enables it to migrate rapidly through the soil while in solution without becoming fixed in place, contributing further to its mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-thompson1to4_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thompson1to4-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atwood4_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atwood4-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Np(V) is also readily absorbed by concrete, which because of the element's radioactivity is a consideration that must be addressed when building <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a> storage facilities. When absorbed in concrete, it is <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduced</a> to Np(IV) in a relatively short period of time. Np(V) is also reduced by <a href="/wiki/Humic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Humic acid">humic acid</a> if it is present on the surface of goethite, <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a>. Np(IV) is absorbed efficiently by <a href="/wiki/Tuff" title="Tuff">tuff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Granodiorite" title="Granodiorite">granodiorite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bentonite" title="Bentonite">bentonite</a>; although uptake by the latter is most pronounced in mildly acidic conditions. It also exhibits a strong tendency to bind to <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloidal particulates</a>, an effect that is enhanced when in soil with a high clay content. The behavior provides an additional aid in the element's observed high mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-thompson1to4_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thompson1to4-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atwood4_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atwood4-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atwood1_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atwood1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_early_claims">Background and early claims</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Background and early claims">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mendelejevs_periodiska_system_1871.png" class="image"><img alt="a table with a typical cell containing a two-letter symbol and a number" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mendelejevs_periodiska_system_1871.png/500px-Mendelejevs_periodiska_system_1871.png" width="500" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/Mendelejevs_periodiska_system_1871.png 1.5x" data-file-width="684" data-file-height="341" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mendelejevs_periodiska_system_1871.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>'s table of 1871, with an empty space at the position after uranium</div></div></div>
<p>When the first <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> of the elements was published by <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a> in the early 1870s, it showed a "&#160;—&#160;" in place after uranium similar to several other places for then-undiscovered elements. Other subsequent tables of known elements, including a 1913 publication of the known radioactive isotopes by <a href="/wiki/Kasimir_Fajans" class="mw-redirect" title="Kasimir Fajans">Kasimir Fajans</a>, also show an empty place after uranium, element 92.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Up to and after the discovery of the final component of the atomic nucleus, the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> in 1932, most scientists did not seriously consider the possibility of elements heavier than uranium. While nuclear theory at the time did not explicitly prohibit their existence, there was little evidence to suggest that they did. However, the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Induced_radioactivity" title="Induced radioactivity">induced radioactivity</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric Joliot-Curie</a> in late 1933 opened up an entirely new method of researching the elements and inspired a small group of Italian scientists led by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> to begin a series of experiments involving neutron bombardment. Although the Joliot-Curies' experiment involved bombarding a sample of <sup>27</sup><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_aluminium" title="Isotopes of aluminium">Al</a> with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> to produce the radioactive <sup>30</sup><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_phosphorus" title="Isotopes of phosphorus">P</a>, Fermi realized that using neutrons, which have no electrical charge, would most likely produce even better results than the positively charged alpha particles. Accordingly, in March 1934 he began systematically subjecting all of the then-known elements to neutron bombardment to determine whether others could also be induced to radioactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After several months of work, Fermi's group had tentatively determined that lighter elements would disperse the energy of the captured neutron by emitting a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> and heavier elements would generally accomplish the same by emitting a <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>. This latter behavior would later result in the <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> of a neutron into a proton, thus moving the resulting isotope one place up the periodic table. When Fermi's team bombarded uranium, they observed this behavior as well, which strongly suggested that the resulting isotope had an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> of 93. Fermi was initially reluctant to publicize such a claim, but after his team observed several unknown half-lives in the uranium bombardment products that did not match those of any known isotope, he published a paper entitled <i>Possible Production of Elements of Atomic Number Higher than 92</i> in June 1934. In it he proposed the name <a href="/wiki/Ausonium" title="Ausonium">ausonium</a> (atomic symbol Ao) for element 93, after the Greek name <i>Ausonia</i> (Italy).<sup id="cite_ref-Fermi_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fermi-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several theoretical objections to the claims of Fermi's paper were quickly raised; in particular, the exact process that took place when an atom <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">captured a neutron</a> was not well understood at the time. This and Fermi's accidental discovery three months later that nuclear reactions could be induced by slow neutrons cast further doubt in the minds of many scientists, notably <a href="/wiki/Aristid_von_Grosse" title="Aristid von Grosse">Aristid von Grosse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ida_Noddack" title="Ida Noddack">Ida Noddack</a>, that the experiment was creating element 93. While von Grosse's claim that Fermi was actually producing <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a> (element 91) was quickly tested and disproved, Noddack's proposal that the uranium had been shattered into two or more much smaller fragments was simply ignored by most because existing nuclear theory did not include a way for this to be possible. Fermi and his team maintained that they were in fact synthesizing a new element, but the issue remained unresolved for several years.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/240px-Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" width="240" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/360px-Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/480px-Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4848" data-file-height="3052" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhenium_single_crystal_bar_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">Rhenium</a>. For a long time, chemists thought that element 93 would be <a href="/wiki/Homology_(chemistry)" title="Homology (chemistry)">homologous</a> to rhenium, making the discovery and identification of neptunium nearly impossible.</div></div></div>
<p>Although the many different and unknown radioactive half-lives in the experiment's results showed that several nuclear reactions were occurring, Fermi's group could not prove that element 93 was being created unless they could isolate it chemically. They and many other scientists attempted to accomplish this, including <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> who were among the best radiochemists in the world at the time and supporters of Fermi's claim, but they all failed. Much later, it was determined that the main reason for this failure was because the predictions of element 93's chemical properties were based on a periodic table which lacked the <a href="/wiki/Actinide_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Actinide series">actinide series</a>. This arrangement placed protactinium below tantalum, uranium below tungsten, and further suggested that element 93, at that point referred to as eka-rhenium, should be similar to the <a href="/wiki/Group_7_element" title="Group 7 element">group 7 elements</a>, including manganese and rhenium. Thorium, protactinium, and uranium, with their dominant oxidation states of +4, +5, and +6 respectively, fooled scientists into thinking they belonged below hafnium, tantalum, and tungsten, rather than below the lanthanide series, which was at the time viewed as a fluke, and whose members all have dominant +3 states; neptunium, on the other hand, has a much rarer, more unstable +7 state, with +4 and +5 being the most stable. Upon finding that <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> and the other transuranic elements also have dominant +3 and +4 states, along with the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/F-block" class="mw-redirect" title="F-block">f-block</a>, the actinide series was firmly established.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the question of whether Fermi's experiment had produced element 93 was stalemated, two additional claims of the discovery of the element appeared, although unlike Fermi, they both claimed to have observed it in nature. The first of these claims was by Czech engineer <a href="/w/index.php?title=Odolen_Koblic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Odolen Koblic (page does not exist)">Odolen Koblic</a> in 1934 when he extracted a small amount of material from the wash water of heated <a href="/wiki/Pitchblende" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitchblende">pitchblende</a>. He proposed the name <a href="/wiki/Bohemium" title="Bohemium">bohemium</a> for the element, but after being analyzed it turned out that the sample was a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Koblic_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koblic-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The other claim, in 1938 by Romanian physicist <a href="/wiki/Horia_Hulubei" title="Horia Hulubei">Horia Hulubei</a> and French chemist <a href="/wiki/Yvette_Cauchois" title="Yvette Cauchois">Yvette Cauchois</a>, claimed to have discovered the new element via <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> in minerals. They named their element <a href="/wiki/Sequanium" title="Sequanium">sequanium</a>, but the claim was discounted because the prevailing theory at the time was that if it existed at all, element 93 would not exist naturally. However, as neptunium does in fact occur in nature in trace amounts, as demonstrated when it was found in uranium ore in 1952, it is possible that Hulubei and Cauchois did in fact observe neptunium.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley345347_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley345347-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fontani_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fontani-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although by 1938 some scientists, including <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>, were still reluctant to accept that Fermi had actually produced a new element, he was nevertheless awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> in November 1938 "<i>for his demonstrations of the existence of new radioactive elements produced by neutron irradiation, and for his related discovery of nuclear reactions brought about by slow neutrons</i>". A month later, the almost totally unexpected discovery of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> by Hahn, Meitner, and <a href="/wiki/Otto_Frisch" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto Frisch">Otto Frisch</a> put an end to the possibility that Fermi had discovered element 93 because most of the unknown half-lives that had been observed by Fermi's team were rapidly identified as <a href="/wiki/Fission_products" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission products">fission products</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Perhaps the closest of all attempts to produce the missing element 93 was that conducted by the Japanese physicist <a href="/wiki/Yoshio_Nishina" title="Yoshio Nishina">Yoshio Nishina</a> working with chemist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenjiro_Kimura&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kenjiro Kimura (page does not exist)">Kenjiro Kimura</a> in 1940, just before the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a> in 1941: they bombarded <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a> with fast neutrons. However, while slow neutrons tend to induce neutron capture through a (n, γ) reaction, fast neutrons tend to induce a "knock-out" (n, 2n) reaction, where one neutron is added and two more are removed, resulting in the net loss of a neutron. Nishina and Kimura, having tested this technique on <sup>232</sup><a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">Th</a> and successfully produced the known <sup>231</sup>Th and its long-lived beta decay daughter <sup>231</sup><a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">Pa</a> (both occurring in the natural decay chain of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a>), therefore correctly assigned the new 6.75-day half-life activity they observed to the new isotope <sup>237</sup>U. They confirmed that this isotope was also a beta emitter and must hence decay to the unknown nuclide <sup>237</sup>93. They attempted to isolate this nuclide by carrying it with its supposed lighter congener rhenium, but no beta or alpha decay was observed from the rhenium-containing fraction: Nishina and Kimura thus correctly speculated that the half-life of <sup>237</sup>93, like that of <sup>231</sup>Pa, was very long and hence its activity would be so weak as to be unmeasurable by their equipment, thus concluding the last and closest unsuccessful search for transuranic elements.<sup id="cite_ref-Ikeda_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ikeda-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Black-and-white picture of heavy machinery with two operators sitting aside" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/170px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" width="170" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/255px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/340px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2409" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 60-inch cyclotron at the Lawrence Radiation Laboratory, University of California, Berkeley, in August 1939</div></div></div>
<p>As research on nuclear fission progressed in early 1939, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_McMillan" title="Edwin McMillan">Edwin McMillan</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Radiation_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Berkeley Radiation Laboratory">Berkeley Radiation Laboratory</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a> decided to run an experiment bombarding uranium using the powerful 60-inch (1.52 m) <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> that had recently been built at the university. The purpose was to separate the various fission products produced by the bombardment by exploiting the enormous force that the fragments gain from their mutual electrical repulsion after fissioning. Although he did not discover anything of note from this, McMillan did observe two new beta decay half-lives in the uranium trioxide target itself, which meant that whatever was producing the radioactivity had not violently repelled each other like normal fission products. He quickly realized that one of the half-lives closely matched the known 23-minute decay period of uranium-239, but the other half-life of 2.3 days was unknown. McMillan took the results of his experiment to chemist and fellow Berkeley professor <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Segr%C3%A8" title="Emilio Segrè">Emilio Segrè</a> to attempt to isolate the source of the radioactivity. Both scientists began their work using the prevailing theory that element 93 would have similar chemistry to rhenium, but Segrè rapidly determined that McMillan's sample was not at all similar to rhenium. Instead, when he reacted it with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a> (HF) with a strong <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizing agent</a> present, it behaved much like members of the <a href="/wiki/Rare_earths" class="mw-redirect" title="Rare earths">rare earths</a>. Since these elements comprise a large percentage of fission products, Segrè and McMillan decided that the half-life must have been simply another fission product, titling the paper "An Unsuccessful Search for Transuranium Elements".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;max-width:262px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photo of Edwin McMillan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg/125px-Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg" width="125" height="177" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg/188px-Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg/250px-Edwin_McMillan_Nobel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="280" data-file-height="396" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:131px;max-width:131px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photo of Philip Abelson" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg/129px-Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg" width="129" height="177" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg/194px-Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg/258px-Philip_Hauge_Abelson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="823" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Neptunium was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_McMillan" title="Edwin McMillan">Edwin McMillan</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Abelson" title="Philip Abelson">Philip Abelson</a> (right) in 1940.</div></div></div>
<p>However, as more information about fission became available, the possibility that the fragments of nuclear fission could still have been present in the target became more remote. McMillan and several scientists, including <a href="/wiki/Philip_H._Abelson" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip H. Abelson">Philip H. Abelson</a>, attempted again to determine what was producing the unknown half-life. In early 1940, McMillan realized that his 1939 experiment with Segrè had failed to test the chemical reactions of the radioactive source with sufficient rigor. In a new experiment, McMillan tried subjecting the unknown substance to HF in the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a>, something he had not done before. This reaction resulted in the sample <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitating</a> with the HF, an action that definitively ruled out the possibility that the unknown substance was a rare earth. 
Shortly after this, Abelson, who had received his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Science" title="Doctor of Science">graduate degree</a> from the university, visited Berkeley for a short vacation and McMillan asked the more able chemist to assist with the separation of the experiment's results. Abelson very quickly observed that whatever was producing the 2.3-day half-life did not have chemistry like any known element and was actually more similar to uranium than a rare earth. This discovery finally allowed the source to be isolated and later, in 1945, led to the classification of the <a href="/wiki/Actinide_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Actinide series">actinide series</a>. As a final step, McMillan and Abelson prepared a much larger sample of bombarded uranium that had a prominent 23-minute half-life from <sup>239</sup>U and demonstrated conclusively that the unknown 2.3-day half-life increased in strength in concert with a decrease in the 23-minute activity through the following reaction:<sup id="cite_ref-EL93_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EL93-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {{^{238}_{92}U}+{^{1}_{0}n}-&gt;{^{239}_{92}U}-&gt;[\beta ^{-}][23\ {\ce {min}}]{^{239}_{93}Np}-&gt;[\beta ^{-}][2.355\ {\ce {days}}]{^{239}_{94}Pu}}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/93b95114fe8847dcf5cd481fc8c5d755b4a1b823" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.127ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.544ex; width:47.953ex; height:6.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {{^{238}_{92}U}+{^{1}_{0}n}-&gt;{^{239}_{92}U}-&gt;[\beta ^{-}][23\ {\ce {min}}]{^{239}_{93}Np}-&gt;[\beta ^{-}][2.355\ {\ce {days}}]{^{239}_{94}Pu}}}}"/></span> <small><i>(The times are <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a>.)</i></small></dd></dl>
<p>This proved that the unknown radioactive source originated from the decay of uranium and, coupled with the previous observation that the source was different chemically from all known elements, proved beyond all doubt that a new element had been discovered. McMillan and Abelson published their results in a paper entitled <i>Radioactive Element 93</i> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Physical_Review" title="Physical Review">Physical Review</a></i> on May 27, 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-EL93_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EL93-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> They did not propose a name for the element in the paper, but they soon decided on the name <i>neptunium</i> since <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> is the next planet beyond <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> in our solar system.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> McMillan and Abelson's success compared to Nishina and Kimura's near miss can be attributed to the favorable half-life of <sup>239</sup>Np for radiochemical analysis and quick decay of <sup>239</sup>U, in contrast to the slower decay of <sup>237</sup>U and extremely long half-life of <sup>237</sup>Np.<sup id="cite_ref-Ikeda_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ikeda-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_developments">Subsequent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Subsequent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neptune_Full.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Neptune_Full.jpg/200px-Neptune_Full.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Neptune_Full.jpg/300px-Neptune_Full.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Neptune_Full.jpg/400px-Neptune_Full.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2200" data-file-height="2200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neptune_Full.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The planet <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, after which neptunium is named</div></div></div>
<p>It was also realized that the beta decay of <sup>239</sup>Np must produce an isotope of element 94 (now called <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>), but the quantities involved in McMillan and Abelson's original experiment were too small to isolate and identify plutonium along with neptunium.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The discovery of plutonium had to wait until the end of 1940, when <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a> and his team identified the isotope <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neptunium's unique radioactive characteristics allowed it to be traced as it moved through various compounds in chemical reactions, at first this was the only method available to prove that its chemistry was different from other elements. As the first isotope of neptunium to be discovered has such a short half-life, McMillan and Abelson were unable to prepare a sample that was large enough to perform chemical analysis of the new element using the technology that was then available. However, after the discovery of the long-lived <sup>237</sup>Np isotope in 1942 by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Seaborg" class="mw-redirect" title="Glenn Seaborg">Glenn Seaborg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wahl" title="Arthur Wahl">Arthur Wahl</a>, forming weighable amounts of neptunium became a realistic endeavor.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-migration_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-migration-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Its half-life was initially determined to be about 3&#160;million&#160;years (later revised to 2.144&#160;million&#160;years), confirming the predictions of Nishina and Kimura of a very long half-life.<sup id="cite_ref-Ikeda_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ikeda-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early research into the element was somewhat limited because most of the nuclear physicists and chemists in the United States at the time were focused on the massive effort to research the properties of plutonium as part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>. Research into the element did continue as a minor part of the project and the first bulk sample of neptunium was isolated in 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-migration_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-migration-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the research into the properties of neptunium since then has been focused on understanding how to confine it as a portion of nuclear waste. Because it has isotopes with very long half-lives, it is of particular concern in the context of designing confinement facilities that can last for thousands of years. It has found some limited uses as a radioactive tracer and a precursor for various nuclear reactions to produce useful plutonium isotopes. However, most of the neptunium that is produced as a reaction byproduct in nuclear power stations is considered to be a waste product.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-migration_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-migration-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neptunium_Purex_process.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Neptunium_Purex_process.png/310px-Neptunium_Purex_process.png" width="310" height="396" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Neptunium_Purex_process.png/465px-Neptunium_Purex_process.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/Neptunium_Purex_process.png 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neptunium_Purex_process.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Flowchart" title="Flowchart">Flowchart</a>, showing the Purex process and the likely oxidation state of neptunium in the process solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida710_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida710-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vast majority of the neptunium that currently exists on Earth was produced in artificial nuclear reactions. Neptunium-237 is the most commonly synthesized isotope due to it being the only one that both can be created via <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> and also has a half-lifelong enough to allow weighable quantities to be easily isolated. As such, it is by far the most common isotope to be utilized in chemical studies of the element.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>When an <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a> atom captures a neutron, it is converted to an excited state of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-236" title="Uranium-236"><sup>236</sup>U</a>. About 81% of the excited <sup>236</sup>U nuclei undergo fission, but the remainder decay to the ground state of <sup>236</sup>U by emitting <a href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma radiation">gamma radiation</a>. Further neutron capture creates <sup>237</sup>U which has a half-life of 7 days and quickly decays to <sup>237</sup>Np through <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>.  During beta decay, the excited <sup>237</sup>U emits an electron, while the atomic <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a> converts a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>, thus creating <sup>237</sup>Np.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{cases}{\ce {^{235}_{92}U + ^{1}_{0}n -&gt; ^{236}_{92}U_m -&gt;[][120 \ {\ce {ns}}] ^{236}_{92}U + \gamma}}\\{\ce {^{236}_{92}U + ^{1}_{0}n -&gt; ^{237}_{92}U -&gt;[\beta^-][6.75 \ {\ce {d}}] ^{237}_{93}Np}}\end{cases}}}">

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/318c46a6a6e1efb57bacd447b7ddad9dd18f9354" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -4.192ex; margin-bottom: -0.646ex; width:39.453ex; height:10.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{cases}{\ce {^{235}_{92}U + ^{1}_{0}n -&gt; ^{236}_{92}U_m -&gt;[][120 \ {\ce {ns}}] ^{236}_{92}U + \gamma}}\\{\ce {^{236}_{92}U + ^{1}_{0}n -&gt; ^{237}_{92}U -&gt;[\beta^-][6.75 \ {\ce {d}}] ^{237}_{93}Np}}\end{cases}}}"/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><sup>237</sup>U is also produced via an (<a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">n</a>,2n) reaction with <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a>. This only happens with very energetic neutrons.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><sup>237</sup>Np is the product of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Americium-241" title="Americium-241"><sup>241</sup>Am</a>, which is produced through neutron irradiation of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Heavier isotopes of neptunium decay quickly, and lighter isotopes of neptunium cannot be produced by neutron capture, so chemical separation of neptunium from cooled <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a> gives nearly pure <sup>237</sup>Np.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The short-lived heavier isotopes <sup>238</sup>Np and <sup>239</sup>Np, useful as <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracers</a>, are produced through neutron irradiation of <sup>237</sup>Np and <sup>238</sup>U respectively, while the longer-lived lighter isotopes <sup>235</sup>Np and <sup>236</sup>Np are produced through irradiation of <sup>235</sup>U with <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuteron" class="mw-redirect" title="Deuteron">deuterons</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida702_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida702-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Artificial <sup>237</sup>Np metal is usually isolated through a reaction of <sup>237</sup>NpF<sub>3</sub> with liquid <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> at around  1200 °<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a> and is most often extracted from spent <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_rod" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear fuel rod">nuclear fuel rods</a> in  kilogram amounts as a by-product in <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> production.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley345347_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley345347-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 NpF<sub>3</sub> + 3 Ba → 2 Np + 3 BaF<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>By weight, neptunium-237 discharges are about 5% as great as plutonium discharges and about 0.05% of <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a> discharges.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> However, even this fraction still amounts to more than fifty tons per year globally.<sup id="cite_ref-rsc_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsc-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purification_methods">Purification methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Purification methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recovering uranium and plutonium from spent nuclear fuel for reuse is one of the major processes of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a>. As it has a long half-life of just over 2&#160;million&#160;years, the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha emitter</a> <sup>237</sup>Np is one of the major isotopes of the <a href="/wiki/Minor_actinide" title="Minor actinide">minor actinides</a> separated from spent nuclear fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida7045_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida7045-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Many separation methods have been used to separate out the neptunium, operating on small and large scales. The small-scale purification operations have the goals of preparing pure neptunium as a <a href="/wiki/Precursor_(chemistry)" title="Precursor (chemistry)">precursor</a> of metallic neptunium and its compounds, and also to isolate and preconcentrate neptunium in samples for analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida7045_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida7045-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most methods that separate neptunium ions exploit the differing chemical behaviour of the differing oxidation states of neptunium (from +3 to +6 or sometimes even +7) in solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida7045_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida7045-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Among the methods that are or have been used are: solvent <a href="/wiki/Extraction_(chemistry)" title="Extraction (chemistry)">extraction</a> (using various <a href="/wiki/Extractant" class="mw-redirect" title="Extractant">extractants</a>, usually <a href="/wiki/Denticity" title="Denticity">multidentate</a> β-diketone derivatives, <a href="/wiki/Organophosphorus_compound" title="Organophosphorus compound">organophosphorus compounds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> compounds), <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a> using various <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion-exchange</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelating</a> resins, <a href="/wiki/Coprecipitation" title="Coprecipitation">coprecipitation</a> (possible <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(chemical_analysis)" title="Matrix (chemical analysis)">matrices</a> include <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lanthanum(III)_fluoride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lanthanum(III) fluoride (page does not exist)">LaF<sub>3</sub></a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth_phosphate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bismuth phosphate (page does not exist)">BiPO<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Barium_sulfate" title="Barium sulfate">BaSO<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_hydroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron(III) hydroxide">Fe(OH)<sub>3</sub></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manganese(IV)_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Manganese(IV) oxide">MnO<sub>2</sub></a>), <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electrodeposition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnological</a> methods.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida70517_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida70517-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Currently, commercial reprocessing plants use the Purex process, involving the solvent extraction of uranium and plutonium with <a href="/wiki/Tributyl_phosphate" title="Tributyl phosphate">tributyl phosphate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida710_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida710-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Chemistry and compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solution_chemistry">Solution chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Solution chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Np_ox_st.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Np_ox_st.jpg/350px-Np_ox_st.jpg" width="350" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/Np_ox_st.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="468" data-file-height="328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Np_ox_st.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neptunium ions in solution</div></div></div>
<p>When it is in an aqueous solution, neptunium can exist in any of its five possible oxidation states (+3 to +7) and each of these show a characteristic color. The stability of each oxidation state is strongly dependent on various factors, such as the presence of <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agents</a>, <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of the solution, presence of <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complex</a>-forming <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>, and even the concentration of neptunium in the solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
