</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Acid_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Acid (chemistry)">acidic</a> solutions, the neptunium(III) to neptunium(VII) ions exist as Np<sup>3+</sup>, Np<sup>4+</sup>, <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>. In <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> solutions, they exist as the oxides and hydroxides Np(OH)<sub>3</sub>, NpO<sub>2</sub>, NpO<sub>2</sub>OH, NpO<sub>2</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>. Not as much work has been done to characterize neptunium in basic solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Np<sup>3+</sup> and Np<sup>4+</sup> can easily be reduced and oxidized to each other, as can <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida759_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida759-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Neptunium(III)</dt></dl>
<p>Np(III) or Np<sup>3+</sup> exists as hydrated complexes in acidic solutions, <span class="chemf nowrap">Np(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It is a dark blue-purple and is analogous to its lighter <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">congener</a>, the pink <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_metal" class="mw-redirect" title="Rare-earth metal">rare-earth</a> ion <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">Pm<sup>3+</sup></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In the presence of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, it is quickly oxidized to Np(IV) unless strong reducing agents are also present. Nevertheless, it is the second-least easily <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolyzed</a> neptunium ion in water, forming the NpOH<sup>2+</sup> ion.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Np<sup>3+</sup> is the predominant neptunium ion in solutions of pH&#160;4–5.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Neptunium(IV)</dt></dl>
<p>Np(IV) or Np<sup>4+</sup> is pale yellow-green in acidic solutions,<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> where it exists as hydrated complexes (<span class="chemf nowrap">Np(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span></span>). It is quite unstable to hydrolysis in acidic aqueous solutions at pH&#160;1 and above, forming NpOH<sup>3+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In basic solutions, Np<sup>4+</sup> tends to hydrolyze to form the neutral neptunium(IV) hydroxide (Np(OH)<sub>4</sub>) and neptunium(IV) oxide (NpO<sub>2</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Neptunium(V)</dt></dl>
<p>Np(V) or <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is green-blue in aqueous solution,<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> in which it behaves as a strong <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> It is a stable ion<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and is the most common form of neptunium in aqueous solutions. Unlike its neighboring homologues <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">PuO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> does not spontaneously <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionate</a> except at very low pH and high concentration:<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida759_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida759-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 4 H<sup>+</sup> ⇌ Np<sup>4+</sup> + <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>It hydrolyzes in basic solutions to form NpO<sub>2</sub>OH and <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Neptunium(VI)</dt></dl>
<p>Np(VI) or <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, the neptunyl ion, shows a light pink or reddish color in an acidic solution and yellow-green otherwise.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It is a strong Lewis acid<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and is the main neptunium ion encountered in solutions of pH&#160;3–4.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Though stable in acidic solutions, it is quite easily reduced to the Np(V) ion,<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and it is not as stable as the homologous hexavalent ions of its neighbours uranium and plutonium (the <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">uranyl</a> and plutonyl ions). It hydrolyzes in basic solutions to form the oxo and hydroxo ions NpO<sub>2</sub>OH<sup>+</sup>, <span class="chemf nowrap">(NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">(NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Neptunium(VII)</dt></dl>
<p>Np(VII) is dark green in a strongly <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> solution. Though its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> in basic solution is frequently cited as <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, this is a simplification and the real structure is probably closer to a hydroxo species like <span class="chemf nowrap">[NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Np(VII) was first prepared in basic solution in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In strongly <a href="/wiki/Acid_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Acid (chemistry)">acidic</a> solution, Np(VII) is found as <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>; water quickly reduces this to Np(VI).<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Its hydrolysis products are uncharacterized.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydroxides">Hydroxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hydroxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The oxides and hydroxides of neptunium are closely related to its ions. In general, Np hydroxides at various oxidation levels are less stable than the actinides before it on the periodic table such as <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and uranium and more stable than those after it such as plutonium and americium. This phenomenon is because the stability of an ion increases as the ratio of atomic number to the radius of the ion increases. Thus actinides higher on the periodic table will more readily undergo <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida753_74-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida753-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neptunium(III) hydroxide is quite stable in acidic solutions and in environments that lack oxygen, but it will rapidly oxidize to the IV state in the presence of air. It is not soluble in water.<sup id="cite_ref-migration_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-migration-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Np(IV) hydroxides exist mainly as the electrically neutral Np(OH)<sub>4</sub> and its mild solubility in water is not affected at all by the pH of the solution. This suggests that the other Np(IV) hydroxide, <span class="chemf nowrap">Np(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, does not have a significant presence.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the Np(V) ion <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is very stable, it can only form a hydroxide in high acidity levels. When placed in a 0.1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration" title="Molar concentration">M</a> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_perchlorate" title="Sodium perchlorate">sodium perchlorate</a> solution, it does not react significantly for a period of months, although a higher molar concentration of 3.0&#160;M will result in it reacting to the solid hydroxide NpO<sub>2</sub>OH almost immediately. Np(VI) hydroxide is more reactive but it is still fairly stable in acidic solutions. It will form the compound NpO<sub>3</sub>· H<sub>2</sub>O in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> under various <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> pressures. Np(VII) has not been well-studied and no neutral hydroxides have been reported. It probably exists mostly as <span class="chemf nowrap">[NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida768_77-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida768-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides">Oxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Oxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Three anhydrous neptunium oxides have been reported, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium(IV)_oxide" title="Neptunium(IV) oxide">NpO<sub>2</sub></a>, Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and Np<sub>5</sub>O<sub>8</sub>, though some studies<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> have stated that only the first two of these exist, suggesting that claims of Np<sub>5</sub>O<sub>8</sub> are actually the result of mistaken analysis of Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. However, as the full extent of the reactions that occur between neptunium and oxygen has yet to be researched, it is not certain which of these claims is accurate. Although neptunium oxides have not been produced with neptunium in oxidation states as high as those possible with the adjacent actinide uranium, neptunium oxides are more stable at lower oxidation states. This behavior is illustrated by the fact that NpO<sub>2</sub> can be produced by simply burning neptunium salts of oxyacids in air.<sup id="cite_ref-lanl_17-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida724_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida724-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sharma_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sharma-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Richteretal_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richteretal-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The greenish-brown NpO<sub>2</sub> is very stable over a large range of pressures and temperatures and does not undergo phase transitions at low temperatures. It does show a phase transition from face-centered cubic to orthorhombic at around 33-37GPa, although it returns to is original phase when pressure is released. It remains stable under oxygen pressures up to 2.84 MPa and temperatures up to 400&#160;°C.  
Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is black-brown in color and <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monoclinic">monoclinic</a> with a lattice size of 418×658×409&#160;picometres. It is relatively unstable and decomposes to NpO<sub>2</sub> and O<sub>2</sub> at 420-695&#160;°C. Although Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was initially subject to several studies that claimed to produce it with mutually contradictory methods, it was eventually prepared successfully by heating neptunium <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxide</a> to 300-350&#160;°C for 2–3 hours or by heating it under a layer of water in an <a href="/wiki/Ampoule" title="Ampoule">ampoule</a> at 180&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida724_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida724-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Richteretal_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richteretal-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neptunium also forms a large number of oxide compounds with a wide variety of elements, although the neptunate oxides formed with <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metals" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkaline earth metals">alkaline earth metals</a> have been by far the most studied. Ternary neptunium oxides are generally formed by reacting NpO<sub>2</sub> with the oxide of another element or by precipitating from an alkaline solution. <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">Li</a><sub>5</sub>NpO<sub>6</sub> has been prepared by reacting Li<sub>2</sub>O and NpO<sub>2</sub> at 400&#160;°C for 16 hours or by reacting Li<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with NpO<sub>3</sub> · H<sub>2</sub>O at 400&#160;°C for 16 hours in a quartz tube and flowing oxygen. Alkali neptunate compounds <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">K</a><sub>3</sub>NpO<sub>5</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">Cs</a><sub>3</sub>NpO<sub>5</sub>, and <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">Rb</a><sub>3</sub>NpO<sub>5</sub> are all created by a similar reaction: 
</p>
<dl><dd>NpO<sub>2</sub> + 3 MO<sub>2</sub> &#8594; M<sub>3</sub>NpO<sub>5</sub>&#160;(M = K, Cs, Rb)</dd></dl>
<p>The oxide compounds KNpO<sub>4</sub>, CsNpO<sub>4</sub>, and RbNpO<sub>4</sub> are formed by reacting Np(VII) (<span class="chemf nowrap">[NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) with a compound of the alkali metal <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>. Additional compounds have been produced by reacting NpO<sub>3</sub> and water with solid alkali and alkaline <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxides</a> at temperatures of 400 - 600&#160;°C for 15–30 hours. Some of these include Ba<sub>3</sub>(NpO<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, Ba<sub>2</sub><a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">Na</a>NpO<sub>6</sub>, and Ba<sub>2</sub>LiNpO<sub>6</sub>. Also, a considerable number of hexavelant neptunium oxides are formed by reacting solid-state NpO<sub>2</sub> with various alkali or alkaline earth oxides in an environment of flowing oxygen. Many of the resulting compounds also have an equivalent compound that substitutes uranium for neptunium. Some compounds that have been characterized include Na<sub>2</sub>Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>NpO<sub>5</sub>, Na<sub>6</sub>NpO<sub>6</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>NpO<sub>4</sub>. These can be obtained by heating different combinations of NpO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>O to various temperature thresholds and further heating will also cause these compounds to exhibit different neptunium allotropes. The lithium neptunate oxides Li<sub>6</sub>NpO<sub>6</sub> and Li<sub>4</sub>NpO<sub>5</sub> can be obtained with similar reactions of NpO<sub>2</sub> and Li<sub>2</sub>O.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida728_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida728-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A large number of additional alkali and alkaline neptunium oxide compounds such as Cs<sub>4</sub>Np<sub>5</sub>O<sub>17</sub> and Cs<sub>2</sub>Np<sub>3</sub>O<sub>10</sub> have been characterized with various production methods. Neptunium has also been observed to form ternary oxides with many additional elements in <a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">groups</a> 3 through 7, although these compounds are much less well studied.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida728_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida728-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neptunium_hexafluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Neptunium hexafluoride">Neptunium hexafluoride</a></div>
<p>Although neptunium <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halide</a> compounds have not been nearly as well studied as its oxides, a fairly large number have been successfully characterized. Of these, neptunium <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluorides</a> have been the most extensively researched, largely because of their potential use in separating the element from nuclear waste products. Four binary neptunium fluoride compounds, Np<a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">F</a><sub>3</sub>, NpF<sub>4</sub>, NpF<sub>5</sub>, and NpF<sub>6</sub>, have been reported. The first two are fairly stable and were first prepared in 1947 through the following reactions:
</p>
<dl><dd>NpO<sub>2</sub> + &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> H<sub>2</sub> + 3 HF &#8594; NpF<sub>3</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O&#160;&#160;&#160;(400°C)</dd>
<dd>NpF<sub>3</sub> + &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> O<sub>2</sub> + HF &#8594; NpF<sub>4</sub> + &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> H<sub>2</sub>O&#160;&#160;(400°C)</dd></dl>
<p>Later, NpF<sub>4</sub> was obtained directly by heating NpO<sub>2</sub> to various temperatures in mixtures of either <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a> or pure fluorine gas. NpF<sub>5</sub> is much more difficult to create and most known preparation methods involve reacting NpF<sub>4</sub> or NpF<sub>6</sub> compounds with various other fluoride compounds. NpF<sub>5</sub> will decompose into NpF<sub>4</sub> and NpF<sub>6</sub> when heated to around 320&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida730_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida730-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drobyetal_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drobyetal-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>NpF<sub>6</sub> or <a href="/wiki/Neptunium_hexafluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Neptunium hexafluoride">neptunium hexafluoride</a> is extremely volatile, as are its adjacent actinide compounds <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> (UF<sub>6</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_hexafluoride" title="Plutonium hexafluoride">plutonium hexafluoride</a> (PuF<sub>6</sub>). This volatility has attracted a large amount of interest to the compound in an attempt to devise a simple method for extracting neptunium from spent nuclear power station fuel rods. NpF<sub>6</sub> was first prepared in 1943 by reacting NpF<sub>3</sub> and gaseous fluorine at very high temperatures and the first bulk quantities were obtained in 1958 by heating NpF<sub>4</sub> and dripping pure fluorine on it in a specially prepared apparatus. Additional methods that have successfully produced neptunium hexafluoride include reacting <a href="/wiki/Bromine_trifluoride" title="Bromine trifluoride">BrF<sub>3</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Bromine_pentafluoride" title="Bromine pentafluoride">BrF<sub>5</sub></a> with NpF<sub>4</sub> and by reacting several different neptunium oxide and fluoride compounds with anhydrous hydrogen fluorides.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida730_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida730-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Four neptunium <a href="/wiki/Oxohalide" title="Oxohalide">oxyfluoride</a> compounds, NpO<sub>2</sub>F, NpOF<sub>3</sub>, NpO<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>, and NpOF<sub>4</sub>, have been reported, although none of them have been extensively studied. NpO<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> is a pinkish solid and can be prepared by reacting NpO<sub>3</sub> · H<sub>2</sub>O and Np<sub>2</sub>F<sub>5</sub> with pure fluorine at around 330&#160;°C.  NpOF<sub>3</sub> and NpOF<sub>4</sub> can be produced by reacting neptunium oxides with anhydrous hydrogen fluoride at various temperatures. Neptunium also forms a wide variety of fluoride compounds with various elements. Some of these that have been characterized include CsNpF<sub>6</sub>, Rb<sub>2</sub>NpF<sub>7</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>NpF<sub>8</sub>, and K<sub>3</sub>NpO<sub>2</sub>F<sub>5</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida730_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida730-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drobyetal_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drobyetal-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two neptunium <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chlorides</a>, Np<a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">Cl</a><sub>3</sub> and NpCl<sub>4</sub>, have been characterized. Although several attempts to create NpCl<sub>5</sub> have been made, they have not been successful. NpCl<sub>3</sub> is created by reducing neptunium dioxide with hydrogen and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a> (<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a>Cl<sub>4</sub>) and NpCl<sub>4</sub> by reacting a neptunium oxide with CCl<sub>4</sub> at around 500&#160;°C. Other neptunium chloride compounds have also been reported, including NpOCl<sub>2</sub>, Cs<sub>2</sub>NpCl<sub>6</sub>, Cs<sub>3</sub>NpO<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>4</sub>, and Cs<sub>2</sub>NaNpCl<sub>6</sub>. Neptunium <a href="/wiki/Bromide" title="Bromide">bromides</a> Np<a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">Br</a><sub>3</sub> and NpBr<sub>4</sub> have also been created; the latter by reacting <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_bromide" title="Aluminium bromide">aluminium bromide</a> with NpO<sub>2</sub> at 350&#160;°C and the former in an almost identical procedure but with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> present. The neptunium <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> Np<a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">I</a><sub>3</sub> has also been prepared by the same method as NpBr<sub>3</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida736_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida736-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chalcogenides.2C_pnictides.2C_and_carbides"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chalcogenides,_pnictides,_and_carbides">Chalcogenides, pnictides, and carbides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Chalcogenides, pnictides, and carbides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neptunium <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogen</a> compounds have been well studied primarily as part of research into their electronic and magnetic properties and their interactions in the natural environment. Pnictide and <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbide</a> compounds have also attracted interest because of their presence in the fuel of several advanced nuclear reactor designs, although the latter group has not had nearly as much research as the former.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida739_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida739-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Chalcogenides</dt></dl>
<p>A wide variety of neptunium <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> compounds have been characterized, including the pure sulfide compounds Np<a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a>, NpS<sub>3</sub>, Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub>, Np<sub>3</sub>S<sub>5</sub>, Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, and Np<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. Of these, Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, prepared by reacting NpO<sub>2</sub> with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> at around 1000&#160;°C, is the most well-studied and three allotropic forms are known. The α form exists up to around 1230&#160;°C, the β up to 1530&#160;°C, and the γ form, which can also exist as Np<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, at higher temperatures. NpS can be created by reacting Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and neptunium metal at 1600&#160;°C and Np<sub>3</sub>S<sub>5</sub> can be prepared by the decomposition of Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at 500&#160;°C or by reacting sulfur and neptunium hydride at 650&#160;°C. Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> is made by heating a mixture of Np<sub>3</sub>S<sub>5</sub> and pure sulfur to 500&#160;°C. All of the neptunium sulfides except for the β and γ forms of Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are <a href="/wiki/Isostructural" title="Isostructural">isostructural</a> with the equivalent uranium sulfide and several, including NpS, α−Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, and β−Np<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are also isostructural with the equivalent plutonium sulfide. The oxysulfides NpOS, Np<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>S, and Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>S have also been created, although the latter three have not been well studied. NpOS was first prepared in 1985 by vacuum sealing NpO<sub>2</sub>, Np<sub>3</sub>S<sub>5</sub>, and pure sulfur in a quartz tube and heating it to 900&#160;°C for one week.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida739_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida739-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pages1985_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pages1985-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neptunium <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a> compounds that have been reported include Np<a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">Se</a>, NpSe<sub>3</sub>, Np<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, Np<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>5</sub>, Np<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>, and Np<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>5</sub>. All of these have only been obtained by heating neptunium hydride and selenium metal to various temperatures in a vacuum for an extended period of time and Np<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> is only known to exist in the γ allotrope at relatively high temperatures. Two neptunium <a href="/wiki/Oxyselenide" title="Oxyselenide">oxyselenide</a> compounds are known, NpOSe and Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>Se, are formed with similar methods by replacing the neptunium hydride with neptunium dioxide. The known neptunium <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">telluride</a> compounds Np<a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">Te</a>, NpTe<sub>3</sub>, Np<sub>3</sub>Te<sub>4</sub>, Np<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>3</sub>, and Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>Te are formed by similar procedures to the selenides and Np<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>Te is isostructural to the equivalent uranium and plutonium compounds. No neptunium−<a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> compounds have been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida739_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida739-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pages1985_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pages1985-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Pnictides and carbides</dt></dl>
<p>Neptunium <a href="/wiki/Nitride" title="Nitride">nitride</a> (Np<a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N</a>) was first prepared in 1953 by reacting neptunium hydride and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> gas at around 750&#160;°C in a quartz capillary tube. Later, it was produced by reacting different mixtures of nitrogen and hydrogen with neptunium metal at various temperatures. It has also been created by the reduction of neptunium dioxide with <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic</a> nitrogen gas at 1550&#160;°C. NpN is <a href="/wiki/Isomorphism_(crystallography)" title="Isomorphism (crystallography)">isomorphous</a> with <a href="/wiki/Uranium_nitride" title="Uranium nitride">uranium mononitride</a> (UN) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plutonium_nitride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Plutonium nitride (page does not exist)">plutonium mononitride</a> (PuN) and has a melting point of 2830&#160;°C under a nitrogen pressure of around 1 MPa. Two neptunium <a href="/wiki/Phosphide" title="Phosphide">phosphide</a> compounds have been reported, Np<a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">P</a> and Np<sub>3</sub>P<sub>4</sub>. The first has a face centered cubic structure and is prepared by converting neptunium metal to a powder and then reacting it with <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a> gas at 350&#160;°C. Np<sub>3</sub>P<sub>4</sub> can be created by reacting neptunium metal with <a href="/wiki/Red_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Red phosphorus">red phosphorus</a> at 740&#160;°C in a vacuum and then allowing any extra phosphorus to <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimate</a> away. The compound is non-reactive with water but will react with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> to produce Np(IV) solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida742_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida742-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fried1953_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fried1953-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Three neptunium <a href="/wiki/Arsenide" title="Arsenide">arsenide</a> compounds have been prepared, Np<a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">As</a>, NpAs<sub>2</sub>, and Np<sub>3</sub>As<sub>4</sub>. The first two were first created by heating arsenic and neptunium hydride in a vacuum-sealed tube for about a week. Later, NpAs was also made by confining neptunium metal and arsenic in a vacuum tube, separating them with a quartz membrane, and heating them to just below neptunium's melting point of 639&#160;°C, which is slightly higher than the arsenic's sublimation point of 615&#160;°C. Np<sub>3</sub>As<sub>4</sub> is prepared by a similar procedure using iodine as a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_transport_reaction" title="Chemical transport reaction">transporting agent</a>. NpAs<sub>2</sub> crystals are brownish gold and Np<sub>3</sub>As<sub>4</sub> is black. The neptunium <a href="/wiki/Antimonide" title="Antimonide">antimonide</a> compound Np<a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">Sb</a> was created in 1971 by placing equal quantities of both elements in a vacuum tube, heating them to the melting point of antimony, and then heating it further to 1000&#160;°C for sixteen days. This procedure also created trace amounts of an additional antimonide compound Np<sub>3</sub>Sb<sub>4</sub>. One neptunium-<a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> compound, NpBi, has also been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida742_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida742-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fried1953_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fried1953-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The neptunium <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a> Np<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a>, Np<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>, and NpC<sub>2</sub> (tentative) have been reported, but have not characterized in detail despite the high importance and utility of actinide carbides as advanced nuclear reactor fuel. NpC is a <a href="/wiki/Non-stoichiometric_compound" title="Non-stoichiometric compound">non-stoichiometric compound</a>, and could be better labelled as NpC<sub><i>x</i></sub> (0.82 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.96). It may be obtained from the reaction of neptunium hydride with <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> at 1400&#160;°C or by heating the constituent elements together in an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> electrode. It reacts with excess carbon to form pure Np<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>. NpC<sub>2</sub> is formed from heating NpO<sub>2</sub> in a graphite crucible at 2660–2800&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida742_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida742-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fried1953_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fried1953-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inorganic">Other inorganic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other inorganic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>Hydrides</dt></dl>
<p>Neptunium reacts with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in a similar manner to its neighbor plutonium, forming the <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a> NpH<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub> (<a href="/wiki/Face-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Face-centered cubic">face-centered cubic</a>) and NpH<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagonal</a>). These are <a href="/wiki/Isostructural" title="Isostructural">isostructural</a> with the corresponding plutonium hydrides, although unlike PuH<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub>, the <a href="/wiki/Lattice_parameter" class="mw-redirect" title="Lattice parameter">lattice parameters</a> of NpH<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub> become greater as the hydrogen content (<i>x</i>) increases. The hydrides require extreme care in handling as they decompose in a vacuum at 300&#160;°C to form finely divided neptunium metal, which is <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoric" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyrophoric">pyrophoric</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida722_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida722-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Phosphates, sulfates, and carbonates</dt></dl>
<p>Being chemically stable, neptunium <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a> have been investigated for potential use in immobilizing nuclear waste. Neptunium pyrophosphate (α-NpP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), a green solid, has been produced in the reaction between neptunium dioxide and <a href="/wiki/Boron_phosphate" title="Boron phosphate">boron phosphate</a> at 1100&#160;°C, though neptunium(IV) phosphate has so far remained elusive. The series of compounds NpM<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, where M is an <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">Li</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">Na</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">Rb</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">Cs</a>), are all known. Some neptunium <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfates</a> have been characterized, both aqueous and solid and at various oxidation states of neptunium (IV through VI have been observed). Additionally, neptunium <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> have been investigated to achieve a better understanding of the behavior of neptunium in <a href="/wiki/Geological_repository" class="mw-redirect" title="Geological repository">geological repositories</a> and the environment, where it may come into contact with carbonate and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> aqueous solutions and form soluble complexes.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida744_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida744-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic">Organometallic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organometallic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/260px-Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" width="260" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/390px-Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png/520px-Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="1092" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neptunocene-from-xtal-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of neptunocene</div></div></div>
<p>A few organoneptunium compounds are known and chemically characterized, although not as many as for <a href="/wiki/Organouranium_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organouranium compound">uranium</a> due to neptunium's scarcity and radioactivity. The most well known organoneptunium compounds are the <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl" title="Cyclopentadienyl">cyclopentadienyl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclooctatetraenyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclooctatetraenyl">cyclooctatetraenyl</a> compounds and their derivatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The trivalent cyclopentadienyl compound Np(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·<a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">THF</a> was obtained in 1972 from reacting Np(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Cl with <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, although the simpler Np(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>) could not be obtained.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Tetravalent neptunium cyclopentadienyl, a reddish-brown complex, was synthesized in 1968 by reacting neptunium(IV) chloride with potassium cyclopentadienide:<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>NpCl<sub>4</sub> + 4&#160;KC<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub> → Np(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>4</sub> + 4&#160;KCl</dd></dl>
<p>It is soluble in <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">THF</a>, and is less sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and water than <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">Pu</a>(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">Am</a>(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Other Np(IV) cyclopentadienyl compounds are known for many <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>: they have the general formula (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>NpL, where L represents a ligand.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Neptunocene" title="Neptunocene">Neptunocene</a>, Np(C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, was synthesized in 1970 by reacting neptunium(IV) chloride with K<sub>2</sub>(C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>). It is <a href="/wiki/Isomorphism_(crystallography)" title="Isomorphism (crystallography)">isomorphous</a> to <a href="/wiki/Uranocene" title="Uranocene">uranocene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Plutonocene">plutonocene</a>, and they behave chemically identically: all three compounds are insensitive to water or dilute bases but are sensitive to air, reacting quickly to form oxides, and are only slightly soluble in benzene and <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Other known neptunium cyclooctatetraenyl derivatives include Np(RC<sub>8</sub>H<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (R = <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butanol" title="Butanol">butanol</a>) and KNp(C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>)·2THF, which is isostructural to the corresponding plutonium compound.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In addition, neptunium <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocarbyl">hydrocarbyls</a> have been prepared, and solvated triiodide complexes of neptunium are a precursor to many organoneptunium and inorganic neptunium compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida750_135-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida750-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_complexes">Coordination complexes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Coordination complexes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is much interest in the <a href="/wiki/Coordination_chemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Coordination chemistry">coordination chemistry</a> of neptunium, because its five oxidation states all exhibit their own distinctive chemical behavior, and the coordination chemistry of the actinides is heavily influenced by the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Actinide_contraction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Actinide contraction (page does not exist)">actinide contraction</a> (the greater-than-expected decrease in <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radii" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionic radii">ionic radii</a> across the actinide series, analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide contraction</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid_state">Solid state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Solid state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Few neptunium(III) coordination compounds are known, because Np(III) is readily oxidized by atmospheric oxygen while in aqueous solution. However, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_formaldehyde_sulfoxylate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium formaldehyde sulfoxylate">sodium formaldehyde sulfoxylate</a> can reduce Np(IV) to Np(III), stabilizing the lower oxidation state and forming various sparingly soluble Np(III) coordination complexes, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">Np<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>·11H<sub>2</sub>O, <span class="chemf nowrap">Np<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>AsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>·H<sub>2</sub>O, and <span class="chemf nowrap">Np<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>[C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)COO]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many neptunium(IV) coordination compounds have been reported, the first one being <span class="chemf nowrap">(Et<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>N)Np(NCS)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>, which is isostructural with the analogous uranium(IV) coordination compound.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Other Np(IV) coordination compounds are known, some involving other metals such as <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CoNp<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">10</sub></span></span>·8H<sub>2</sub>O, formed at 400&#160;K) and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CuNp<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">10</sub></span></span>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, formed at 600&#160;K).<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Complex nitrate compounds are also known: the experimenters who produced them in 1986 and 1987 produced single crystals by slow evaporation of the Np(IV) solution at ambient temperature in concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> and excess 2,2′-<a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The coordination chemistry of neptunium(V) has been extensively researched due to the presence of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cation%E2%80%93cation_interaction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cation–cation interaction (page does not exist)">cation–cation interactions</a> in the solid state, which had been already known for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Actinyl&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Actinyl (page does not exist)">actinyl</a> ions.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Some known such compounds include the neptunyl <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span></span>·8H<sub>2</sub>O and neptunium glycolate, both of which form green crystals.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neptunium(VI) compounds range from the simple oxalate <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> (which is unstable, usually becoming Np(IV)) to such complicated compounds as the green <span class="chemf nowrap">(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Extensive study has been performed on compounds of the form <span class="chemf nowrap">M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>AnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, where M represents a monovalent cation and An is either uranium, neptunium, or plutonium.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1967, when neptunium(VII) was discovered, some coordination compounds with neptunium in the +7 oxidation state have been prepared and studied. The first reported such compound was initially characterized as <span class="chemf nowrap">Co(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O in 1968, but was suggested in 1973 to actually have the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">[Co(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>][NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>]</span>·2H<sub>2</sub>O based on the fact that Np(VII) occurs as <span class="chemf nowrap">[NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> in aqueous solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> This compound forms dark green prismatic crystals with maximum edge length 0.15–0.4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Millimeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Millimeter">mm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida745_136-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida745-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_aqueous_solution">In aqueous solution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neptunium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: In aqueous solution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most neptunium <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> known in solution involve the element in the +4, +5, and +6 oxidation states: only a few studies have been done on neptunium(III) and (VII) coordination complexes.<sup id="cite_ref-Yoshida77182_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yoshida77182-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> For the former, NpX<sup>2+</sup> and <span class="chemf nowrap">NpX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (X = <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">Cl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">Br</a>) were obtained in 1966 in concentrated <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">LiCl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_bromide" title="Lithium bromide">LiBr</a> solutions, respectively: for the latter, 1970 experiments discovered that the <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ion could form <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> complexes in acidic solutions, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">NpO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup
